Each panel carries its own surface-brightness colour bar and a $100$\,kpc scale bar.}
\label{fig:iclimg}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{Program Scope and Synergy}

The program is scoped as a cluster-dark-matter mapping and LSB-census effort. The deliverables are a uniform UDG catalog with structural parameters and stellar-population estimates across the target clusters, deep ICL maps that serve as luminous dark-matter proxies for cross-comparison with weak- and strong-lensing mass reconstructions, and a sample of intracluster compact emission-line tracers for ensemble dynamics. The natural synergies are with Euclid and Rubin/LSST, which provide the wide but shallower imaging that selects target clusters and feeds weak-lensing mass maps, and with the strong-lens program of Section~4.2, whose mass models supply the ground truth against which the ICL-as-tracer hypothesis is tested. High-resolution spectroscopy from large ground-based telescopes remains the source of individual UDG velocity dispersions. The mission's role is the deep, wide, space-stable diffuse-light measurement that no ground-based facility can match. Taken together, the inner ICL shape compared against lensing, the merger offsets of the collisionless stellar component, the globular-cluster and planetary-nebula dynamics, and the outer profile to the splashback radius constitute a dark-matter map of each target cluster spanning from the core to the accretion boundary, with the self-interaction cross section constrained at cluster velocities to complement the dwarf-scale measurement. The concrete deliverables are the following.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.4em,itemsep=1pt,topsep=2pt]
\item A uniform ultra-diffuse-galaxy catalog across $\sim20$--$40$ clusters with structural parameters, stellar-population estimates, and globular-cluster counts.
\item A field LSBG and UDG catalog over the full $100$--$300$\,deg$^2$ wide footprint to $\mu_{5\sigma}(10'')\simeq29.8$--$31.3$ in $g$ (Table~\ref{tab:lsbsurvey}) with slitless redshifts, physical sizes, and survey-internal environments for the emission-line subset.
\item Deep intracluster-light maps to $\mu\sim29$--$30$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$ serving as luminous proxies for the projected dark-matter distribution from the core to the splashback radius.
\item Collisionless-tracer centroids and shapes for merger-offset and halo-ellipticity constraints on $\sigma/m$ at cluster velocities.
\item Ensemble kinematics of intracluster planetary nebulae and globular clusters for the diffuse-component dynamics.
\item Internal cross-checks against weak- and strong-lensing mass maps built from background sources in the same fields.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Dwarf Galaxies and the Particle Nature of Dark Matter}

Dwarf galaxies are the smallest gravitationally bound stellar systems known. They are the most dark-matter-dominated objects in the Universe. The faintest Milky Way satellites have dynamical mass-to-light ratios of hundreds to thousands in solar units. Because their shallow potentials and low star-formation efficiencies make them maximally sensitive to the microphysics of dark matter and to the baryonic feedback that competes with it, they are the decisive testing ground for whether dark matter is cold and collisionless, self-interacting, or wave-like. This program is a wide-field search for dwarf galaxies, in the field, in groups, and around nearby hosts, designed to build the uniform samples on which the small-scale tests of dark matter depend and to characterize them spectroscopically.

\subsubsection{Dwarfs as Dark-Matter Laboratories}

The dark-matter content of a pressure-supported dwarf is measured from the line-of-sight stellar velocity dispersion $\sigma_{\rm los}$ and the projected half-light radius $r_{1/2}$ through the mass estimator
\begin{equation}
M_{1/2}=\frac{4\,\sigma_{\rm los}^2\,r_{1/2}}{G}\simeq930\left(\frac{\sigma_{\rm los}}{\rm km\,s^{-1}}\right)^{2}\!\left(\frac{r_{1/2}}{\rm pc}\right)M_\odot,
\label{eq:wolf}
\end{equation}
which is robust to the unknown velocity anisotropy because it is evaluated at the half-light radius \cite{wolf2010}. Applied to the classical and ultra-faint dwarf spheroidals, Eq.~\eqref{eq:wolf} yields masses that exceed the stellar mass by one to four orders of magnitude. The ultra-faint dwarfs in particular, with luminosities as low as a few hundred $L_\odot$ and velocity dispersions of $2$--$10$ km\,s$^{-1}$, are the most dark-matter-dominated systems known and the cleanest probes of the halo mass function and inner density structure at the smallest accessible scales \cite{mateo1998,simon2019}. Their abundance, radial distribution, and internal densities each encode different aspects of the dark matter model.

\subsubsection{Small-Scale Challenges to Cold Dark Matter}

Collisionless cold dark matter (CDM) reproduces the observed Universe on large scales but faces a set of persistent tensions on dwarf-galaxy scales, reviewed comprehensively by \cite{bullock2017}. The \emph{core--cusp} problem is the mismatch between the steep central density cusps, $\rho\propto r^{-1}$, predicted by dissipationless CDM simulations and the shallower, cored profiles inferred from the rotation curves and stellar kinematics of many dwarfs \cite{deblok2010}. The \emph{missing-satellites} problem is the order-of-magnitude excess of predicted subhalos over observed satellites \cite{moore1999}. The \emph{too-big-to-fail} problem is the observation that the densest predicted subhalos are too centrally dense to host the observed bright satellites \cite{boylankolchin2011}. Most sharply, the \emph{diversity} problem is the wide scatter in the inner slopes of dwarf rotation curves at fixed maximum circular velocity, with galaxies ranging from strongly cored to cuspy where CDM predicts near-uniformity \cite{oman2015}. Baryonic feedback can plausibly erase cusps in gas-rich dwarfs with extended star formation, but it struggles to explain the gas-poor and ultra-faint regime and the full breadth of the diversity, which keeps open the possibility that the resolution lies in the dark sector itself.

\subsubsection{Self-Interacting Dark Matter}

Self-interacting dark matter (SIDM) was proposed to address exactly these tensions by endowing dark matter with an elastic self-scattering cross section \cite{spergel2000}. Scattering transports heat into the halo center. The rate per particle, $\Gamma\simeq(\sigma/m)\,\rho\,v_{\rm rms}$, becomes of order one over a Hubble time when $\sigma/m\sim1$ cm$^2$\,g$^{-1}$ at dwarf-halo densities and velocities, naturally forming a constant-density core \cite{tulinyu2018}. A particularly powerful feature is that dwarf halos act as ``particle colliders.'' The inferred core sizes and central densities map directly to the cross section at the corresponding relative velocity. Therefore, a velocity-dependent $\sigma/m$ can be reconstructed across systems from dwarfs to clusters \cite{kaplinghat2016}. SIDM also predicts a second, counter-intuitive phase. After the cored stage, a halo can undergo gravothermal core collapse to a high central density, so that the same physics produces both unusually diffuse and unusually dense dwarfs. This bimodal outcome offers a natural explanation of the rotation-curve diversity that uniform CDM does not \cite{ren2019}. Testing SIDM therefore requires not a single dwarf but the \emph{distribution} of central densities across a large, uniformly selected dwarf sample, spanning a range of halo masses and environments, which is what a wide-field census provides. This observational program is the empirical counterpart of the mission team's companion cosmological SIDM and wave-dark-matter simulations, which predict the core-size and central-density distributions to be confronted with the data.

\subsubsection{Fuzzy and Wave Dark Matter}

If dark matter is instead an ultralight boson with $m\sim10^{-22}$\,eV, its de~Broglie wavelength is of kiloparsec scale and wave (``fuzzy'') effects suppress small-scale structure and replace central cusps with solitonic cores \cite{hu2000,schive2014}. The soliton core radius follows a core--halo relation that scales as
\begin{equation}
r_{\rm c}\simeq1.6\;{\rm kpc}\left(\frac{m}{10^{-22}\,\rm eV}\right)^{-1}\!\left(\frac{M_{\rm halo}}{10^{9}\,M_\odot}\right)^{-1/3},
\label{eq:soliton}
\end{equation}
so that dwarf galaxies, with the smallest halo masses, host the largest and most observable cores and set the strongest constraints on the boson mass \cite{schive2014}. Figure~\ref{fig:fdmslice} shows what such a halo looks like in the first cosmological wave-dark-matter simulation with de~Broglie interference granules pervading the halo and filaments and the solitonic core standing at the center in place of a cusp \cite{schive2014}. Stellar-kinematic analyses of individual dwarfs give boson masses clustering near $m\sim(1$--$6)\times10^{-22}$\,eV \cite{calabrese2016}, while the same systems used as a population place these models in tension with the simultaneous existence of dense and diffuse dwarfs. As with SIDM, the discriminating measurement is statistical, requiring core sizes and densities inferred across many dwarfs rather than one. Figure~\ref{fig:soliton} draws the relation of Eq.~\eqref{eq:soliton} across the halo-mass range of the census together with the kinematically preferred boson-mass band, showing that the predicted cores of dwarf-scale halos span hundreds of parsecs to kiloparsecs and therefore imprint directly on the sizes and central surface brightnesses the survey measures.

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{fdm_wave_slice.jpg}
\caption{A galaxy-scale halo in the first cosmological simulation of wave (fuzzy) dark matter, shown as a density slice with successive zooms. The kiloparsec de~Broglie wavelength of the ultralight boson imprints interference granules and destructive fringes throughout the filaments and the halo. The halo center hosts the distinctive solitonic core of Eq.~\eqref{eq:soliton} rather than a cusp. These cores, largest in the smallest halos, are the structure that the dwarf census tests at the population level. [courtesy: Schive et al. 2014 \cite{schive2014}]}
\label{fig:fdmslice}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.72\textwidth]{fdm_soliton_relation.png}
\caption{The published fuzzy-dark-matter core--halo relation of Eq.~\eqref{eq:soliton} \cite{schive2014} at $z=0$ for four boson masses with the band preferred by dwarf-spheroidal kinematics \cite{calabrese2016}. In the dwarf halo-mass range (shaded) the predicted soliton cores reach a substantial fraction of the galaxy size. Hence, the size and central-surface-brightness distributions of a uniform dwarf census constrain the boson mass at the population level.}
\label{fig:soliton}
\end{figure}

The kinematically preferred masses are themselves under pressure from independent probes. Figure~\ref{fig:fdmconstraints} compiles the published bounds. The Ly$\alpha$ forest excludes $m<2\times10^{-21}$\,eV even with a conservative thermal history \cite{irsic2017fdm} and $m<2\times10^{-20}$\,eV once the intergalactic thermal history is marginalized \cite{rogers2021}, the Milky Way satellite census requires $m>2.9\times10^{-21}$\,eV \cite{nadler2021}, and the sizes and stellar kinematics of Segue~1 and Segue~2 exclude everything below $3\times10^{-19}$\,eV \cite{dalal2022}. Taken at face value these bounds close the window in which the boson explains the dwarf cores, but they rest on different astrophysical assumptions and different systems from the kinematic preferences. What settles the question is a single uniform dwarf census whose core-size and central-density distributions confront the preferred band directly, which is precisely the population-level measurement the survey delivers.

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{fdm_mass_constraints.png}
\caption{Published constraints on the fuzzy-dark-matter boson mass, drawn from the quoted papers. Green bands mark the masses preferred by stellar kinematics of dwarf spheroidals \cite{schive2014} and of the ultra-faint dwarfs Draco~II and Triangulum~II \cite{calabrese2016}. Red regions are excluded by the Ly$\alpha$ forest under conservative thermal-history assumptions \cite{irsic2017fdm}, by the Milky Way satellite census \cite{nadler2021}, by the Ly$\alpha$ forest marginalized over the intergalactic thermal history \cite{rogers2021}, and by the sizes and stellar kinematics of Segue~1 and 2 \cite{dalal2022}. The direct conflict between the kinematically preferred band and the exclusion limits is the current state of the field. The uniform core-size census of this survey tests the preferred band on the very population that produces it.}
\label{fig:fdmconstraints}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{The Halo Mass Function as a Dark-Matter Probe}

Beyond the inner density structure, the sheer \emph{abundance} of low-mass halos is a sharp discriminant between dark-matter models. Cold dark matter predicts a halo mass function that continues as a power law to far below the dwarf scale, whereas any model with a finite small-scale cutoff truncates it. Warm dark matter does so through the free-streaming of a thermal relic. Fuzzy dark matter does so through the de~Broglie wavelength that suppresses power below a half-mode scale. For a thermal warm relic the suppression enters at dwarf and satellite masses for particle masses of a few keV. For fuzzy dark matter the cutoff sits at the dwarf scale for $m\sim10^{-22}$\,eV. Therefore, the faint-galaxy regime is exactly where the models diverge. The census of Milky Way satellites already turns this into a quantitative bound. The observed number and luminosity function of satellites discovered by the Dark Energy Survey and Pan-STARRS, corrected for completeness, set lower limits on the thermal warm-dark-matter mass and constrain the suppression scale of fuzzy and interacting models \cite{nadler2021}. The two observables are the faint end of the galaxy luminosity function, which counts the halos that managed to form stars, and the radial distribution of satellites around their hosts, which is additionally sensitive to the survival of substructure. Self-interacting dark matter, for instance, accelerates the tidal evaporation and core collapse of subhalos and so alters both their counts and their central densities. A wide-field survey with a controlled selection function across many host environments is the instrument that measures both.

\subsubsection{Disentangling Baryonic Feedback from Dark-Sector Cores}

The central difficulty in interpreting dwarf densities is that supernova feedback can also transform cusps into cores by repeatedly moving gas in and out of the center, mimicking a dark-sector signal. The two explanations are separable, however, because they predict different \emph{correlations}. Feedback-driven cores require a substantial integrated star-formation history to supply the energy. Hence, the core size should correlate with stellar mass and with the duration of star formation. The mechanism becomes ineffective in the gas-poor and ultra-faint dwarfs that formed too few stars to inject the necessary energy. Therefore, a large core in such a system points to the dark sector \cite{deblok2010,bullock2017}. Self-interacting dark matter instead ties the core size to the halo properties and the scattering cross section rather than to the baryons and uniquely predicts the coexistence of diffuse cored and dense core-collapsed dwarfs at fixed mass, the signature behind its explanation of the rotation-curve diversity \cite{kaplinghat2016,ren2019}. Fuzzy dark matter predicts a solitonic core whose radius follows the halo-mass relation of Eq.~\eqref{eq:soliton}, a different functional dependence again. The discriminating measurement is therefore the joint distribution of central density, stellar mass, star-formation history, and environment across a large and uniformly selected dwarf sample, rather than the rotation curve of any single galaxy. Predicting these distributions for each dark-matter model is the role of the mission team's companion cosmological simulations, which evolve self-interacting and wave (fuzzy) dark matter at high resolution and output precisely the core-size and central-density distributions to be confronted with the observed census. Table~\ref{tab:dmmodels} summarizes the competing models, their published defining scales and current constraints, and the census observable that tests each.

\begin{table}[tp]
\centering
\sffamily\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}p{1.3cm}p{4.6cm}p{4.4cm}X@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Defining scale and constraint} & \textbf{Dwarf-scale signature} & \textbf{Census observable}\\
\midrule
CDM & Collisionless at all scales \cite{bullock2017} & $\rho\propto r^{-1}$ cusps and an unbroken low-mass halo mass function \cite{deblok2010,moore1999} & Faint-end abundance and a near-uniform inner-structure population in tension with the observed diversity \cite{oman2015}\\
WDM & Thermal relic of a few keV, half-mode mass $M_{\rm hm}\sim10^{8}$--$10^{10}\,M_\odot$, bounded from below by the Milky Way satellite census \cite{nadler2021} & Turnover and deficit at the faint end of the halo and galaxy mass functions & Faint-end luminosity functions in field and void environments with one selection function\\
SIDM & $\sigma/m\sim1$ cm$^2$\,g$^{-1}$ at dwarf velocities \cite{spergel2000,tulinyu2018}, $\lesssim0.5$ cm$^2$\,g$^{-1}$ at cluster velocities \cite{harvey2015} & Coexisting diffuse cored dwarfs and gravothermally collapsed dense dwarfs at fixed mass \cite{kaplinghat2016,ren2019} & Distribution of central density and size across the census, plus the outlier target list for kinematic follow-up\\
FDM & Ultralight boson with $m\sim10^{-22}$ eV, kinematic estimates $(1$--$6)\times10^{-22}$ eV \cite{hu2000,schive2014,calabrese2016} & Soliton cores largest in the smallest halos (Eq.~\eqref{eq:soliton}, Figure~\ref{fig:soliton}) plus a mass-function cutoff & Core-size trend with halo mass jointly with the faint-end abundance\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\caption{The competing dark-matter models, their published defining parameters and current constraints, the dwarf-scale signatures they predict, and the observable of the uniform census that tests each. The quantitative predictions for every row are supplied by the mission team's companion simulations.}
\label{tab:dmmodels}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{Chemical Evolution as a Halo and Reionization Probe}

The integrated spectra and multi-band photometry of the survey deliver stellar metallicities, ages, and star-formation histories. These chemical diagnostics are themselves a dark-matter probe. Local Group dwarfs follow a tight stellar mass--metallicity relation across many decades in luminosity, with the faintest systems the most metal-poor \cite{kirby2013}. The relation is set by the depth of the potential well, since deeper halos retain a larger fraction of the metals their supernovae produce against galactic winds, and by the timing of reionization, whose ultraviolet background truncated star formation in the shallowest halos before they could enrich. The mean metallicity of a dwarf is therefore a fossil record of its halo mass and of when it stopped forming stars, information that complements the present-day dynamics and that the mission measures from integrated light even where individual-star spectroscopy is impossible.

This chemical information sharpens the abundance test of the preceding subsection in a specific way. If the faint end of the galaxy population is suppressed because low-mass halos exist but were quenched at reionization, the surviving ultra-faint systems should be uniformly old and extremely metal-poor, lying on the extrapolation of the mass--metallicity relation. If instead the low-mass halos are genuinely absent, as warm or fuzzy dark matter would have it, the faint population is simply missing rather than old and metal-poor. A uniform metallicity and age census across a wide range of environments thus helps distinguish a star-formation explanation of the missing-satellite and void phenomena from a true truncation of the halo mass function, tying the chemical evolution of dwarfs to the particle nature of dark matter. The same data also locate the most metal-poor, and hence most pristine and most dark-matter-dominated, systems as priority targets for the high-resolution kinematic follow-up that measures their central densities.

\subsubsection{A Starlight-Independent Halo Census from Strong Lensing}

The deepest difficulty in counting low-mass halos through their galaxies is that one only ever counts the halos that lit up. Gravitational lensing removes this limitation by detecting halos through their mass alone, whether or not they contain stars. Gravitational imaging of extended Einstein rings reveals individual dark subhalos as localized distortions of the lensed surface brightness, a technique that has detected a dark satellite of mass $\sim2\times10^{8}\,M_\odot$ in a lens galaxy \cite{vegetti2012}. High-resolution interferometry has localized a comparable subhalo in the lensed system SDP.81 \cite{hezaveh2016}. Flux-ratio and astrometric anomalies in multiply imaged quasars provide a complementary, statistical route to the same low-mass population. Because these methods measure the abundance and concentration of halos independently of their luminous content, they test directly whether the low-mass halos predicted by cold dark matter exist at all, breaking the degeneracy with star formation that limits the luminous dwarf census.

This is where the dwarf program and the strong-lens program of Section~4.2 reinforce each other. The strong-lens survey supplies the lens sample and the machine-learning modeling pipeline needed to detect substructure at scale, yielding a halo census from the dark side, while the wide-field dwarf survey yields the census from the luminous side with the controlled selection function and the stellar-population and chemical diagnostics that say which halos formed stars and when. Confronting the two against the same dark-matter models closes the loop. Agreement between the luminous and lensing halo mass functions would confirm that the faint-galaxy deficit is a star-formation effect within abundant cold-dark-matter halos, while a luminous deficit not matched by a lensing deficit would point to a genuine truncation of the mass function by warm or fuzzy dark matter. Neither probe alone is decisive, but together they are, and the mission carries both.

\subsubsection{The Galaxy--Halo Connection at the Threshold of Galaxy Formation}

The faintest dwarfs sit at the threshold where galaxy formation itself shuts off. The shape of the galaxy--halo connection there is a joint probe of baryonic physics and the dark-matter model. The relation between stellar mass and halo mass, nearly a power law for massive galaxies, steepens dramatically toward low mass and develops large scatter, reflecting the increasing inefficiency and stochasticity of star formation in shallow potentials and the suppression of star formation by the reionization ultraviolet background below a critical halo mass. Whether there exists a minimum halo mass for galaxy formation, and how the stellar mass scatters about the halo mass near it, are predictions that the ultra-faint regime tests directly \cite{simon2019,bullock2017}. The observable is the faint end of the galaxy stellar-mass function together with the abundance and radial distribution of satellites, which the satellite census already uses to constrain both galaxy formation and the dark-matter free-streaming scale \cite{nadler2021}.

A uniform wide-field census is what turns this into a quantitative measurement. Present samples of faint dwarfs are assembled from many surveys with incompatible depths and selection criteria, which is the dominant systematic in any galaxy--halo or abundance analysis, whereas a single instrument with one selection function across field, group, and host environments pins the faint-end stellar-mass function and its environmental dependence. The mission's contribution is therefore not a kinematic measurement of any one halo but the population-level mapping of the galaxy--halo connection at its faint extreme, anchored by the structural, stellar-population, and chemical diagnostics that the same data provide and feeding both the abundance test of the dark-matter cutoff and the inner-density tests of self-interacting and fuzzy models. This is the counterpart in the luminous sector to the lensing halo census. It is the regime where the predictions of the companion simulation program are most distinctive.

\subsubsection{Field-Dwarf Rotation Curves and the Diversity Problem}

The sharpest current statement of the small-scale tension comes from the rotation curves of gas-rich field dwarfs and low-surface-brightness disks, for which homogeneous compilations such as the SPARC database of mass models provide spatially resolved inner kinematics for nearly two hundred galaxies \cite{lelli2016}. These data show that at a fixed maximum circular velocity the inner rotation curves span the full range from strongly cored to cuspy, the diversity problem in its cleanest form \cite{oman2015}. They are the benchmark against which cold, self-interacting, and fuzzy dark-matter predictions are tested. Figure~\ref{fig:sparcdiv} shows the problem in the observed data in a pair of SPARC dwarfs of nearly the same outermost rotation speed whose inner curves differ by a factor of four in velocity. The interpretation, however, is limited by the heterogeneity of the existing samples, which are drawn from many surveys with different selection criteria, distance scales, and environments, so that intrinsic scatter is entangled with selection and systematic effects.

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.72\textwidth]{sparc_diversity.png}
\caption{The rotation-curve diversity problem in the observed data, drawn directly from the public SPARC mass-model files \cite{lelli2016}. IC~2574 and UGC~5721 reach nearly the same outermost rotation speed, $67$ and $79$\,km\,s$^{-1}$. Hence, they occupy halos of comparable mass, yet at $2$\,kpc one rotates at $20$ and the other at $82$\,km\,s$^{-1}$, a factor of four in velocity and more than an order of magnitude in enclosed mass. Reproducing both extremes at fixed halo mass is the benchmark for the dark-matter models of Table~\ref{tab:dmmodels}. Self-interacting dark matter fits both galaxies with a single cross section \cite{kaplinghat2016,ren2019}.}
\label{fig:sparcdiv}
\end{figure}

This is where a uniform, wide-field dwarf census contributes even though it does not itself resolve the few-tens-of-km\,s$^{-1}$ internal kinematics. The mission identifies the gas-rich field, group, and void dwarfs that are the targets for resolved rotation-curve work. It measures their structural parameters, stellar populations, and environments on a single system. It also supplies the systemic redshifts and the environmental context that the rotation-curve interpretation requires. The resolved inner kinematics themselves come from integral-field and radio follow-up, but the value of those data is set by the quality and uniformity of the parent sample and of its environmental characterization, which is exactly what the mission provides. A diversity measurement built on a controlled, space-selected parent sample, spanning environments from voids to clusters, is far more constraining for the dark-matter models than the same rotation curves drawn from a heterogeneous catalog.

\subsubsection{What an $R=1000$ Slitless Survey Contributes}

A slitless survey at $R=1000$ cannot resolve the few-km\,s$^{-1}$ internal velocity dispersions of dwarfs, which lie a factor of thirty below its resolution element and remain the province of high-resolution multi-object spectroscopy. The mission's contribution is the discovery and characterization that gate the small-scale dark-matter tests. A deep wide-field imaging and grism survey detects LSB dwarfs and ultra-diffuse galaxies to faint magnitudes over large contiguous areas. It confirms systemic redshifts and group or host membership from emission lines in the gas-rich systems and from strong stellar absorption features in the brighter ones. It measures integrated stellar populations, metallicities, and star-formation histories that separate the formation channels relevant to the dark-matter interpretation. Crucially, it delivers a \emph{uniform, well-understood selection function} over a wide area, removing the single greatest systematic limitation of present dwarf samples, their heterogeneous assembly from many surveys with incompatible depths and criteria. The resulting catalogs, with structural parameters and stellar-population diagnostics, feed both the high-resolution kinematic follow-up that measures central densities and the gravitational-lensing substructure analyses that count low-mass halos independently of their starlight. Table~\ref{tab:dwarfreach} summarizes the program.

\begin{table}[tp]
\centering
\sffamily\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}p{3.5cm}p{3.3cm}X@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Measurement} & \textbf{Method at $R=1000$} & \textbf{Dark-matter test it enables}\\
\midrule
Dwarf/UDG census & Deep imaging + grism, wide field & Halo mass function, and abundance versus CDM, WDM, and FDM suppression\\
Membership and distance & Emission/absorption redshifts, SBF, TRGB from imaging & Clean environmental and host association and selection function\\
Stellar populations & Integrated metallicity, age, SFH & Formation channel, baryonic versus dark-sector core formation\\
Structural diversity & Sizes, central surface brightness & Distribution of inner densities (SIDM core/collapse, FDM solitons)\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\caption{Planning reach of the dwarf-galaxy program. The mission delivers the wide-field discovery, membership, and stellar-population characterization with a uniform selection function. Internal stellar velocity dispersions are below the $R=1000$ resolution and require high-resolution follow-up, while lensing substructure provides a starlight-independent halo census.}
\label{tab:dwarfreach}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{Program Scope}

The dwarf program is scoped as a wide-field census plus spectroscopic characterization. The adopted plan combines the wide-survey imaging and grism data, used to identify and structurally measure dwarfs and UDGs over the survey footprint, with deeper targeted fields around selected nearby hosts and groups to push the luminosity limit into the ultra-faint regime. The deliverables are a uniform dwarf and UDG catalog with structural and stellar-population parameters and a controlled selection function, redshift confirmation and membership for the gas-rich and brighter systems, and a target list of central-density outliers for high-resolution kinematic follow-up. The science return is a population-level test of CDM, SIDM, and fuzzy dark matter through the abundance, radial distribution, and structural diversity of the lowest-mass galaxies in direct comparison with the companion simulation program.

The program is deliberately structured as a multi-probe attack on a single question, the particle nature of dark matter, rather than a collection of unrelated dwarf measurements. The abundance and radial distribution of dwarfs test the halo mass function and its small-scale cutoff. The structural and chemical diagnostics separate baryon-driven from dark-sector core formation. The strong-lens substructure census counts halos independently of their starlight. The void, field, group, and cluster sub-samples, observed with a single selection function, control the environmental systematics that have confounded previous comparisons. Each probe alone is suggestive and degenerate. The value lies in their combination over one homogeneous catalog, confronted with the predicted distributions from the team's high-resolution self-interacting and wave dark-matter simulations. The mission is the only element of this chain that delivers the wide-field, space-based, uniformly selected census on which every downstream measurement depends. It does so as a by-product of survey data taken for the flagship program, making the dark-matter return of the dwarf census highly cost-effective. The concrete deliverables are the following.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.4em,itemsep=1pt,topsep=2pt]
\item A uniform dwarf and ultra-diffuse-galaxy catalog with structural parameters, stellar metallicities, ages, and a controlled selection function across field, group, host, and void environments.
\item The faint-end galaxy stellar-mass function and the satellite radial distributions that constrain the halo mass function and its warm or fuzzy cutoff.
\item The joint distribution of central density, stellar mass, star-formation history, and environment that separates baryon-driven from SIDM and FDM core formation.
\item A prioritized target list of central-density and metallicity outliers for high-resolution kinematic follow-up.
\item A starlight-independent halo census from the strong-lens substructure program of Section~4.2, confronted against the luminous census within the same dark-matter models.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Dark Galaxies in Cosmic Voids}

Cosmic voids are the vast underdense regions, tens of megaparsecs across, that occupy most of the volume of the Universe. Their interiors are the closest thing to a pristine cosmological laboratory. Galaxies there evolve in near-isolation, free of the mergers, ram pressure, and tidal harassment that reshape galaxies in clusters, so that the imprint of the dark matter model on galaxy formation is least contaminated by environment. Voids are also where the predictions of cold dark matter are hardest to reconcile with observation, because CDM populates them with abundant low-mass halos that appear to host few visible galaxies. This program searches the interiors of nearby voids for the faint, gas-rich, and optically dark galaxies that test whether those halos exist and whether they form stars, exploiting the mission's emission-line sensitivity to objects that have almost no continuum light.

\subsubsection{The Void Phenomenon}

The tension is long-standing. Cold dark matter predicts that voids are not empty of halos but are filled with low-mass ones, yet the observed void galaxy population is sparse and, where present, consists of faint late-type dwarfs. The mismatch between the many predicted small halos and the few observed void galaxies is the ``void phenomenon'' \cite{peebles2001}. Two broad resolutions are possible. They point in opposite directions for the nature of dark matter. In the first, the low-mass halos exist but failed to form stars, because the ultraviolet background and inefficient gas cooling suppressed star formation below a halo-mass threshold \cite{hoeft2010}. Voids then contain a population of gas-bearing but optically dark or ultra-faint systems waiting to be found. In the second, the low-mass halos are themselves suppressed, as warm or fuzzy dark matter would predict through the truncation of the halo mass function below a free-streaming or de~Broglie scale. Because voids are the most sensitive environment to the abundance of low-mass halos, a complete census of their faint galaxy content discriminates directly between these possibilities.

\subsubsection{Dark and Almost-Dark Galaxies}

A dark galaxy is a dark-matter halo that contains gas but few or no stars. Several candidates and ``almost-dark'' systems are now known. The prototype is VIRGOHI\,21, an H\,\textsc{i} cloud in the Virgo region showing apparent rotation but no optical counterpart, originally interpreted as a massive dark object \cite{minchin2005}. Blind H\,\textsc{i} surveys with Arecibo (ALFALFA) systematically isolated the small fraction of H\,\textsc{i} sources lacking obvious optical counterparts. The most extreme example, ``Coma\,P'' (HI1232+20), is a metal-poor H\,\textsc{i} cloud with an H\,\textsc{i}-to-stellar mass ratio exceeding $200$ and only a trace very-low-surface-brightness stellar component \cite{janowiecki2015}. A larger related population is the H\,\textsc{i}-bearing ultra-diffuse galaxies. ALFALFA identified $115$ isolated, gas-rich, extremely diffuse field galaxies, bluer and more irregular than their cluster counterparts and apparently inhabiting high-spin dwarf halos with very low star-formation efficiency \cite{leisman2017}. These systems share the property that makes them invisible to continuum imaging but ideal for the present mission. They are gas-rich and forming at least some stars and, therefore, they emit nebular lines while contributing almost no continuum flux. Table~\ref{tab:darkbench} collects these benchmark systems and the place each occupies in the census.

\begin{table}[tp]
\centering
\sffamily\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}p{3.2cm}p{6.4cm}X@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{System} & \textbf{Published properties} & \textbf{Place in the census}\\
\midrule
H\,\textsc{i}-bearing UDGs & 115 isolated, gas-rich, extremely diffuse field systems in high-spin dwarf halos with very low star-formation efficiency \cite{leisman2017} & Line-emitting gas-rich dwarfs, detected directly through H$\alpha$ and [O\,\textsc{iii}]\\
Coma\,P (HI1232$+$20) & Metal-poor H\,\textsc{i} cloud with $M_{\rm HI}/M_\star>200$ and only a trace very-low-surface-brightness stellar body \cite{janowiecki2015} & Almost-dark class, weak lines with an H\,\textsc{i} counterpart\\
Nube & Stellar mass of the Small Magellanic Cloud spread so thinly it evaded standard imaging, discussed as a possible fuzzy-dark-matter signature \cite{montes2024nube} & Almost-dark class, recovered by the deep-imaging stack (Figure~\ref{fig:nube})\\
VIRGOHI\,21 & Rotating H\,\textsc{i} structure with no optical counterpart, later discussed as a candidate tidal feature \cite{minchin2005,duc2008} & The false positive that the isolation and dynamical-mass vetting removes\\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\caption{Benchmark systems along the almost-dark sequence and the class each occupies in the three-way classification of the census. The genuinely dark end of the sequence, gas halos with neither lines nor continuum, is the population the census is designed to isolate.}
\label{tab:darkbench}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{Voids as a Clean Probe of Dark Matter}

The void environment sharpens every one of these tests. Systematic surveys of void galaxies, such as the Void Galaxy Survey, find them to be preferentially small, blue, gas-rich, late-type systems, consistent with slow, accretion-dominated assembly in isolation \cite{kreckel2012}. There is, moreover, kinematic evidence that void dwarfs carry a higher ratio of dark-matter halo mass to stellar mass than dwarfs in denser regions, exactly the signature expected if star formation is suppressed in low-mass void halos \cite{douglass2019}. A void-targeted search thus addresses the nature of dark matter on two fronts at once. The faint end of the void luminosity and H\,\textsc{i} mass functions measures the abundance of low-mass halos in the regime where warm and fuzzy dark matter most strongly suppress structure, while the gas-rich, dark-matter-dominated void dwarfs provide low-baryon, low-environment systems whose inner density structure tests SIDM and FDM with the baryonic and tidal confusion of cluster dwarfs removed.

\subsubsection{Voids as a Cosmological and Small-Halo Probe}

Voids are a cosmological probe in their own right. Their abundance, sizes, shapes, and the velocity field of the galaxies streaming out of them are sensitive to the growth rate of structure, the equation of state of dark energy, and the summed neutrino mass. Void dynamics has been used to constrain cosmology and gravity directly \cite{hamaus2016}. For the \emph{nature} of dark matter, voids have a special role that denser environments lack. Because there is no surrounding overdensity to repopulate them, the abundance of low-mass halos inside a void is not replenished by accretion. Hence, a small-scale cutoff in the power spectrum from warm or fuzzy dark matter shows up there most cleanly and with the least astrophysical contamination. The faint-end slope of the void galaxy luminosity function, and of the void H\,\textsc{i} mass function, is thus among the most direct measurements of the low-mass halo abundance available. A deficit relative to the cold-dark-matter prediction would point to a truncated mass function rather than to a star-formation effect, especially when combined with the H\,\textsc{i} census that detects the gas in halos that failed to form stars. The cooling physics is quantitatively understood. Photoheating by the ultraviolet background suppresses gas accretion and star formation below a halo mass of a few $\times10^{9}\,M_\odot$. Therefore, the predicted population of optically dark or ultra-faint gas halos is concentrated exactly at the scale where the dark-matter models also differ \cite{hoeft2010}.

\subsubsection{Building the Census from Almost-Dark to Dark}

The known almost-dark systems already show what such a census would contain. Beyond Coma\,P, the ALFALFA survey has uncovered objects such as AGC\,229101, a two-billion-solar-mass H\,\textsc{i} cloud with only a very-low-surface-brightness optical counterpart, and the recently identified ``Nube,'' an almost-dark dwarf with the stellar mass of the Small Magellanic Cloud spread so thinly that it evaded detection in ordinary imaging. The extreme diffuseness of Nube has been discussed as a possible signature of fuzzy dark matter \cite{montes2024nube}. Figure~\ref{fig:nube} shows why such objects went unfound for so long. Nube is invisible at the depth of the SDSS, barely present at the depth of Stripe 82, and unambiguous only at $\mu_r\simeq30.5$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$, the surface-brightness regime the mission reaches over wide contiguous areas as a matter of course. These objects populate a sequence from gas-rich ultra-diffuse galaxies with measurable star formation, through almost-dark clouds with trace stellar populations, to the still-hypothetical genuinely dark gas halos. The mission's selection is well placed along it. The line-luminosity limits of Table~\ref{tab:llumlim} translate directly into a star-formation-rate sensitivity through the standard calibration ${\rm SFR}\simeq7.9\times10^{-42}\,(L_{\rm H\alpha}/{\rm erg\,s^{-1}})\,M_\odot\,{\rm yr^{-1}}$ \cite{kennicutt1998}. A wide-tier H$\alpha$ luminosity limit of a few $\times10^{41}$ erg\,s$^{-1}$ at the redshifts of nearby voids corresponds to star-formation rates well below $10^{-2}\,M_\odot\,{\rm yr^{-1}}$, deep into the regime of the faintest gas-rich dwarfs. The emission-line detections, which require only ongoing star formation and no detectable continuum, are then cross-matched against H\,\textsc{i} maps. Systems seen in both are gas-rich star-forming void dwarfs, systems seen in H\,\textsc{i} with a faint line counterpart are the almost-dark population, and systems seen in H\,\textsc{i} with no emission lines and no continuum are the candidate genuinely dark halos that the void phenomenon predicts. This three-way classification is the core deliverable. It is only possible by combining a continuum-blind emission-line survey with a gas survey.

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.82\textwidth]{nube_discovery.png}
\caption{The almost-dark galaxy Nube, adapted from the discovery paper \cite{montes2024nube} (arXiv source package). The main panel is the SDSS RGB image of the $20'\times20'$ field, in which the galaxy is invisible. The insets show the same object at $3\sigma$ $r$-band limiting depths of $26.7$, $28.6$, and $30.5$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$ over $10''\times10''$ boxes (SDSS, IAC Stripe 82, and HiPERCAM). An object with the stellar mass of the Small Magellanic Cloud emerges only in the deepest frame, which is the surface-brightness regime this mission reaches over wide contiguous areas (Section~\ref{sec:lsb} and Figure~\ref{fig:iclimg}).}
\label{fig:nube}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{The Warm and Fuzzy Dark-Matter Test in Voids}

The quantitative target of the void census is the small-scale cutoff in the halo mass function. Warm dark matter from a thermal relic of mass $m_X$ suppresses structure below a half-mode mass that falls in the dwarf-to-satellite range, $M_{\rm hm}\sim10^{8}$--$10^{10}\,M_\odot$ for $m_X$ of a few keV. Fuzzy dark matter with $m\sim10^{-22}$\,eV imposes a comparable cutoff through its de~Broglie scale. Both predict a turnover and then a deficit at the faint end of the mass function relative to scale-free cold dark matter \cite{schive2014,bullock2017}. The Milky Way satellite census already places these models near the dwarf scale \cite{nadler2021}, but the satellite measurement is made in the special, tidally violent environment of a massive host. Voids provide the complementary and in some respects cleaner measurement, because their underdensity is not replenished by accretion and the low-mass halos there are neither tidally stripped nor environmentally quenched by a hot intracluster medium. The faint-end slope of the void galaxy luminosity function, together with the void H\,\textsc{i} mass function, therefore measures the surviving low-mass halo abundance in the regime where the dark-matter models diverge, with the star-formation-threshold physics of these halos under good theoretical control \cite{hoeft2010}. The mission team's companion warm and fuzzy dark-matter simulations predict the void galaxy and H\,\textsc{i} mass functions for each model and free-streaming or boson mass. Hence, the measured faint-end abundance maps onto a constraint on the cutoff scale.

\subsubsection{Void Galaxies as Isolated Formation Laboratories}

Quite apart from the abundance test, the void galaxies that are found are valuable because of how they formed. The Void Galaxy Survey established that void galaxies are preferentially small, blue, gas-rich, late-type systems, many showing morphological and kinematic signatures of ongoing cold-gas accretion and small H\,\textsc{i}-rich companions, consistent with slow, merger-free assembly in isolation \cite{kreckel2012}. They tend to be more metal-poor at fixed stellar mass than their counterparts in denser regions. There is kinematic evidence that they carry a higher ratio of dark-matter halo mass to stellar mass \cite{douglass2019}, both expected if star formation proceeds slowly and inefficiently in shallow, isolated potentials. This makes void dwarfs nearly ideal targets for the inner-structure tests of dark matter. They are strongly dark-matter-dominated, they have experienced minimal tidal disturbance that could be confused with a dark-sector core. Their extended, undisturbed gas disks are exactly the systems whose rotation curves most cleanly expose the core--cusp distinction and the rotation-curve diversity discussed in Section~4.6. A void-selected sample thus serves the dark-matter program twice over. Its faint-end statistics constrain the halo mass function. Its individual gas-rich members are the cleanest available laboratories for the inner density structure that distinguishes cold, self-interacting, and fuzzy dark matter.

\subsubsection{Survey Design and Target Voids}

The program is built around a set of nearby voids selected from the void catalogs constructed from the SDSS and DESI redshift surveys, which identify underdense regions and their boundaries from the galaxy distribution with well-defined geometric algorithms \cite{sutter2012}. Restricting the primary search to voids at $z\lesssim0.05$ keeps the diagnostic nebular lines, H$\alpha$ and the [O\,\textsc{iii}] and [O\,\textsc{ii}] doublets, within the most sensitive part of the band and places the targets at distances where even very low-mass dwarfs are spatially resolved. At these redshifts the line-luminosity limits of Section~3.4 reach star-formation rates well below $10^{-2}\,M_\odot\,{\rm yr^{-1}}$. Therefore, the survey is complete to the actively star-forming dwarfs that dominate the void population and reaches into the regime of the faintest known gas-rich systems. Tiling the interiors of a sample of large voids to the wide- and medium-tier depths yields a volume-complete emission-line census whose faint end measures the void galaxy luminosity and star-formation-rate functions with a uniform, space-based selection that ground-based void surveys, assembled heterogeneously and limited by atmospheric systematics, cannot match.

The void measurement also stands on its own as cosmology. The abundance, sizes, and shapes of voids, and the outflow velocities of the galaxies that trace their walls, constrain the growth rate of structure, the dark-energy equation of state, and the summed neutrino mass. Void dynamics has already been used to place competitive constraints on cosmology and gravity \cite{hamaus2016}. A spectroscopic survey that places accurate redshifts on faint galaxies deep inside voids improves both the geometric definition of the voids and the interior tracer density, sharpening these dynamical measurements while simultaneously delivering the faint-galaxy census for the dark-matter test. The H\,\textsc{i} cross-match that separates star-forming from genuinely dark gas halos uses current radio data from ALFALFA, Apertif, and MeerKAT and will deepen substantially with the SKA. Hence, the optically dark population isolated by the mission defines a target list that grows in value as the radio facilities advance. The combination of a uniform optical emission-line census, an internal cosmological measurement from void dynamics, and a radio-defined dark-halo target list is what makes the void program more than a search for individual curiosities.

\subsubsection{Environment as a Control across Void, Field, and Cluster Dwarfs}

The scientific power of a void sample is amplified by what it controls for. A dwarf in a cluster has been tidally stripped, ram-pressure swept, and harassed. A dwarf in a group has likely interacted with neighbors. Therefore, any inference about the dark-matter inner structure from such a system must disentangle these environmental effects from the dark-sector signal. A void dwarf has experienced essentially none of them, having assembled slowly and in isolation. Hence, it provides the zero-environment baseline against which the field, group, and cluster populations can be compared at fixed halo mass. Differences that persist after this comparison are intrinsic and those that vanish were environmental. This is decisive for the small-scale dark-matter tests, because tidal stripping can lower a halo's central density in a way that superficially resembles a self-interaction core. Only an undisturbed comparison sample can separate the two.

The void population is also the cleanest test of galaxy formation in low-density dark-matter environments. Void galaxies are observed to be gas-rich, blue, and actively accreting, with extended undisturbed disks \cite{kreckel2012}, to be more metal-poor at fixed stellar mass, and to carry a higher ratio of halo mass to stellar mass than their denser-environment counterparts \cite{douglass2019}, all consistent with slow, inefficient, accretion-dominated growth in shallow isolated potentials whose star formation is throttled by the cooling threshold \cite{hoeft2010}. Because the mission observes void, field, group, and cluster dwarfs with the same instrument and the same selection function, it can place all four environments on a common footing and measure the environmental dependence of the galaxy--halo connection and of the inner density structure without the cross-survey systematics that have limited previous comparisons. The void sample thus serves simultaneously as the abundance probe of the halo mass function, the source of the cleanest individual dark-matter-dominated laboratories, and the control environment that calibrates the environmental systematics of the entire dwarf program.

\subsubsection{Robust Identification of the Genuinely Dark Population}

Claiming a genuinely dark galaxy is notoriously difficult, because the leading false positive is tidal debris. Gas pulled out of interacting galaxies can form rotating H\,\textsc{i} structures and even tidal dwarf galaxies that mimic an isolated rotating disk yet contain little or no dark matter. Several early dark-galaxy candidates were reinterpreted this way \cite{duc2008}. A credible census must therefore combine several discriminants rather than rely on an H\,\textsc{i} detection alone. Isolation is the first. A candidate deep in a void, far from any plausible parent, cannot easily be tidal debris, which is precisely why voids are the right place to search. The second is the dynamical mass budget. An H\,\textsc{i} line width that implies a dynamical mass far exceeding the gas-plus-stars mass signals a dark halo, whereas tidal debris is baryon-dominated. The third is the deep optical non-detection, where the platform's stable, airglow-free imaging sets the most stringent surface-brightness limits and, by stacking many candidates, can detect or rule out a stellar component an order of magnitude fainter than any individual frame.

The mission's emission-line search adds a fourth, complementary discriminant. A genuinely dark halo by definition lacks the recent star formation that would produce nebular lines. Therefore, the signature of the darkest systems is an H\,\textsc{i} detection with \emph{no} accompanying line emission and no continuum, while the almost-dark systems show faint lines from a trace of star formation. Ordering the void population along this sequence, from line-emitting gas-rich dwarfs, through almost-dark clouds with weak lines, to H\,\textsc{i}-only halos with neither lines nor continuum, is what converts a heterogeneous list of curiosities into a quantitative measurement of how galaxy formation fails at the lowest halo masses. The combination of isolation, dynamics, deep imaging limits, and emission-line non-detection is far more robust than any single criterion. The mission supplies three of the four directly.

\subsubsection{Detection with Deep Slitless Spectroscopy}

Continuum-selected imaging surveys, even deep ones, miss gas-rich star-poor systems precisely because their stellar continuum is faint, which is why most dark-galaxy candidates have been found in blind H\,\textsc{i} surveys rather than optically. A blind slitless spectroscopic survey inverts the selection in a complementary way. It detects galaxies by their emission lines, namely H$\alpha$, [O\,\textsc{iii}], and [O\,\textsc{ii}], independently of continuum brightness. Hence, it is intrinsically sensitive to objects with high line-to-continuum ratios. A modest amount of ongoing star formation in an otherwise optically dark void halo produces detectable line emission against a negligible continuum, which is the easiest possible target for grism spectroscopy. Applied to the volumes of nearby voids, the line-flux depths of Section~3.4 (Table~\ref{tab:snrelg}) translate into star-formation-rate limits well into the dwarf regime. Hence, the survey recovers the actively star-forming part of the void halo population that continuum imaging cannot. Cross-matching the emission-line detections with deep H\,\textsc{i} maps from current and future radio surveys (ALFALFA, Apertif, MeerKAT, and ultimately the SKA) then completes the gas-plus-stars accounting and isolates the genuinely dark, gas-only halos as the systems detected in H\,\textsc{i} but absent in line emission. This complementarity between an optical line survey that selects on star formation and a radio survey that selects on neutral gas is what makes the full inventory of low-mass halos accessible, since neither selection alone reaches the whole population. Table~\ref{tab:voidreach} summarizes the strategy.

The calculator turns the line-flux depth into an explicit star-formation-rate floor. Figure~\ref{fig:darkgal} gives the exposure to a $5\sigma$ H$\alpha$ detection as a function of star-formation rate for a galaxy in a nearby void at $z=0.05$, using the standard Kennicutt calibration. The survey reaches $\sim3\times10^{-3}\,M_\odot\,{\rm yr}^{-1}$ in about an hour and $\sim10^{-3}\,M_\odot\,{\rm yr}^{-1}$ in a deep pointing. Therefore, the actively star-forming part of the void-halo population is recovered well into the dwarf regime.

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.68\textwidth]{darkgal_exptime_vs_sfr.png}
\caption{Exposure to a $5\sigma$ H$\alpha$ detection versus star-formation rate for a void galaxy at $z=0.05$, from the Kennicutt SFR calibration. The $1$ and $12$\,hr levels are marked.}
\label{fig:darkgal}
\end{figure}

\begin{table}[tp]
\centering
\sffamily\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}p{3.6cm}p{3.2cm}X@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Target class} & \textbf{Selection} & \textbf{Dark-matter use}\\
\midrule
Star-forming void dwarfs & Blind emission lines (H$\alpha$, [O\,\textsc{iii}]) & Faint end of the void luminosity function and low-mass halo abundance versus WDM and FDM\\
Almost-dark / HI-bearing UDGs & Lines + H\,\textsc{i} cross-match & High gas-to-star systems and the star-formation threshold in low-mass halos\\

\caption{Planning strategy of the void dark-galaxy program. The mission's emission-line selection is sensitive to gas-rich, continuum-faint systems that continuum imaging misses. Cross-matching with H\,\textsc{i} surveys separates star-forming from genuinely dark gas halos.}
\label{tab:voidreach}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{Program Scope}

The void program is scoped as a deep, volume-complete emission-line search in selected nearby voids identified from existing redshift surveys, combined with archival and forthcoming H\,\textsc{i} coverage. The deliverables are the faint end of the void galaxy luminosity and star-formation-rate functions, a catalog of almost-dark and H\,\textsc{i}-bearing ultra-diffuse void systems with stellar-population constraints, and a target list of optically dark H\,\textsc{i} halos for deeper study. The scientific return is a measurement of the abundance and star-forming state of low-mass halos in the lowest-density environment, which is simultaneously the strongest test of warm and fuzzy dark matter through the small-scale halo abundance and a clean, isolation-selected sample for the SIDM and FDM inner-structure tests of Section~4.6.

The program is also a long-lived legacy resource. The optically dark and almost-dark systems it isolates define the target list for the deep H\,\textsc{i} follow-up that the SKA and its precursors will make routine. Hence, the value of the catalog grows as the radio facilities advance. The void census provides the low-density anchor for the environmental comparisons of the dwarf program of Section~4.6. Because the search exploits the same emission-line sensitivity, multi-roll depth, and wide field that the flagship survey already delivers, the void volumes are covered at marginal additional cost. The resulting faint-end galaxy and star-formation-rate functions, the catalog of almost-dark and H\,\textsc{i}-bearing ultra-diffuse void systems, and the list of candidate genuinely dark halos together constitute a measurement of how and whether galaxy formation operates at the lowest halo masses in the emptiest regions of the Universe. That is the regime in which cold, warm, fuzzy, and self-interacting dark matter make their most divergent predictions and where the cleanest environment offers the least astrophysical confusion to obscure them. The concrete deliverables are the following.
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.4em,itemsep=1pt,topsep=2pt]
\item A volume-complete emission-line census of selected nearby voids yielding the faint-end void galaxy luminosity and star-formation-rate functions.
\item A catalog of almost-dark and H\,\textsc{i}-bearing ultra-diffuse void systems with stellar-population and structural constraints.
\item A list of candidate genuinely dark H\,\textsc{i} halos, isolation- and dynamics-vetted against tidal-debris contamination for deep H\,\textsc{i} follow-up with the SKA and its precursors.
\item Improved interior tracer densities for void-dynamics cosmology.
\item A measurement of the void faint-end abundance confronted with the warm and fuzzy dark-matter predictions of the companion simulation program.
\item A low-density control sample, observed with identical selection and instrumentation for the environmental comparisons of the dwarf dark-matter program.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Wide Survey Mode}

The wide survey uses the \fovlarge\ field, two band-limited grism settings per wavelength channel, and three orientations. The practical unit of observation is therefore a bundle that contains direct imaging, grism exposures at the three roll angles of $0^\circ$, $45^\circ$, and $90^\circ$, dithers, and calibration overhead. Every survey time estimate in this document quotes all of these components rather than a single ``exposure per pointing''.

Table~\ref{tab:timeline} gives the resulting observing-program envelope. The year ranges are not a fixed schedule but an existence proof, showing that commissioning, the truth fields, the wide ELG map, the calibration tiers, and the companion programs fit together within a multi-year robotic survey.

The wide survey targets two high-Galactic-latitude caps that avoid the Milky Way and overlap the existing wide surveys, chosen together with the legacy deep fields they enclose as described in Section~\ref{sec:deepfields}.

\subsection{Deep and Time-Domain Mode}

The \fovsmall\ mode is valuable for deeper fields where the science is not area-limited, such as supernova cadence fields, the targeted intracluster-light cluster program of Section~\ref{sec:lsb}, deep calibration fields, quasar monitoring, and algorithm-validation fields with existing spectroscopy. It should not define the wide RSD survey unless the mission accepts a much smaller cosmological footprint.

\clearpage
\section{Flat Fielding and the Cirrus Foreground in the Imaging Surveys}\label{sec:flatcirrus}

Deep surface photometry is a mission-wide concern rather than a property of any single program. The same two systematic terms govern the direct imaging of the wide ELG tiers, the imaging-only wide extensions, the two ultra-deep fields, and the targeted cluster program of Section~\ref{sec:lsb}. Therefore, the control strategy is collected here once with the ultra-deep fields as the most demanding case.

Surface photometry at $\mu\gtrsim29$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$ is limited by two instrumental-and-foreground terms that must be controlled together, the multiplicative flat field and the additive diffuse foregrounds. The flat-field requirement is severe because the science signal sits so far below the sky. A residual illumination error of one percent on the $\mu_g\simeq22$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$ zodiacal background imprints a false diffuse signal at $\mu_g\simeq27$, more than four magnitudes above the ultra-deep limit of $31.5$. Hence, the low-order flat must be controlled to the $10^{-4}$ level over the field.

The techniques that reach this level are established across the deep surveys. They share one principle, that the trusted low-order reference is the sky and the stars rather than any internal lamp, because a lamp or dome screen illuminates the optics differently from the sky and carries its own scattered-light pattern. The oldest of these methods is the star flat, in which repeated photometry of the same stars stepped across the focal plane solves directly for the position-dependent photometric response \cite{manfroid1996}. It remains the working correction of a modern wide-field mosaic, since the Dark Energy Camera detrending applies exactly such star-flat solutions on top of its dome flats and uses the internal consistency of on-sky observations to separate quantum efficiency, pixel-size variation, and stray light \cite{bernstein2017pasp}. The SDSS generalised the idea to the survey scale as photometric self-calibration (\"ubercalibration), a single least-squares solution over all overlapping scans that reached one-percent relative calibration in $griz$ over $8500$\,deg$^2$ \cite{padmanabhan2008}. Survey-design simulations show that what makes such a solution converge is precisely the strategy of returning the same sources to very different focal-plane positions \cite{holmes2012}. For the diffuse regime itself the working reference is the dark night sky. The deepest wide-aperture ground photometry to date, $\mu_r=31.5$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$ ($3\sigma$, $10\times10$\,arcsec$^2$) at the 10.4-m GTC, was flattened with dark-sky flats built from the dithered science exposures themselves \cite{trujillofliri2016}. At the hardware end, tunable monochromatic illumination measured against calibrated photodiodes transfers a laboratory throughput standard to the full telescope-plus-detector system, the route proposed for percent-level ground-based photometry \cite{stubbs2006}.

The adopted strategy combines these methods on the space platform, using the sky itself as the flat source. Lamp-style internal flats calibrate only the high-frequency pixel response. The low-order illumination is measured from the science data as a dark-sky superflat, the median of the many dithered exposures of the survey with all sources masked, which works in space because the zodiacal light is smooth on the field scale and does not fluctuate with time the way airglow does. The large-amplitude dithers and the three roll angles of the survey separate detector-fixed patterns from sky-fixed signal by construction, the same redundancy the slitless deblending already requires. The overlap of neighbouring tiles admits the joint least-squares solution for the multiplicative flat and the additive sky that the \"ubercalibration introduced \cite{padmanabhan2008}. The stability of a space platform is what makes this converge, since the same star field revisited at different rolls and offsets ties every pixel to a common photometric zero point.

The Galactic cirrus enters differently, as a real additive foreground rather than a calibration error. As anticipated, it is not negligible even in the cleanest fields. At the field-mean $857$\,GHz intensities of the chosen ultra-deep fields the scattered-light scaling of Section~\ref{sec:etc} predicts diffuse galactic light at $\mu_g\simeq27.2$ and $\mu_H\simeq26.7$ mag\,arcsec$^{-2}$ \cite{ienaka2013}, several magnitudes brighter than the depth limit. Therefore, it is the \emph{structure} of the cirrus rather than its mean level that sets the true confusion floor for the diffuse science. Figure~\ref{fig:cirrusfil} shows that structure directly, the same cirrus filament imaged by \emph{Herschel} at $250\,\mu$m and in deep optical data, where the dust appears at higher signal-to-noise than in the far-infrared map that conventionally traces it \cite{roman2020}. Three controls are applied. First, the field choice itself is the strongest lever, because the cirrus fluctuation power scales roughly as the cube of the mean brightness \cite{gautier1992}. Hence, observing at a quarter of the median sky brightness suppresses the fluctuation power by a factor of order fifty relative to typical high-latitude sky. Second, the Planck $857$\,GHz and IRAS $100\,\mu$m maps provide an external template of the structured cirrus, scaled to the optical and near-infrared through the measured $b(\lambda)$ ratios \cite{ienaka2013}, which subtracts the large-scale component and flags the residual regions where diffuse detections should be distrusted. Third, colour discriminates the survivors, since the scattered-light spectrum of the cirrus differs from galaxy light, the measured cirrus colours sitting bluer in $r-i$ at a given $g-r$ than any stellar population (Figure~\ref{fig:cirruscolors}) \cite{roman2020}. Cirrus knots masquerading as LSBGs are a known contaminant that ground-based LSB catalogs must vet against \cite{zaritsky2023}, a vetting the five-band photometry performs object by object.

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\textwidth]{cirrus_optical_ir.jpg}
\caption{The same Galactic cirrus imaged in the far infrared and in deep optical data, the \emph{Herschel}/SPIRE $250\,\mu$m map (top) and the deep SDSS Stripe82 $r$-band mosaic (bottom) with the full Moon placed for angular scale. The deep optical imaging detects the cirrus at higher signal-to-noise than the far-infrared map that is conventionally used to trace it, which is why the structured cirrus, rather than its smooth mean level, sets the confusion floor for diffuse science at the depths of this proposal and why the infrared template must be supplemented by controls in the survey's own bands. [courtesy: Rom\'an, Trujillo \& Montes 2020 \cite{roman2020}]}
\label{fig:cirrusfil}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[tp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{cirrus_colors.png}
\caption{Optical colour separation of Galactic cirrus from extragalactic light. The grey diamonds are the measured colours of cirrus fields in the deep SDSS Stripe82 data, the orange contours enclose $68\%$ and $95\%$ of the pixels of real galaxies, and the coloured tracks are single-stellar-population models spanning age and metallicity. The cirrus sits bluer in $r-i$ at a given $g-r$ than any stellar population, with the dashed line in the left panel separating the dust regime from the extragalactic one. This is the object-by-object vetting that the five-band survey photometry applies to every diffuse detection. [courtesy: Rom\'an, Trujillo \& Montes 2020 \cite{roman2020}]}
\label{fig:cirruscolors}
\end{figure}

\clearpage
\section{Expected Deliverables}

The mission will release science-ready products rather than only calibrated images:
\begin{itemize}
  \item direct-image source catalogs with morphology parameters and astrometric covariance;
  \item calibrated 2D slitless exposures for each filter, roll, and dispersion direction;
  \item extracted 1D spectra with contamination models and covariance;
  \item probabilistic redshift catalogs with line-identification likelihoods;
  \item survey masks, random catalogs, and completeness maps for clustering analyses;
  \item supernova host-scene models and binned spectral time series;
  \item high-redshift quasar continuum fits and damping-wing likelihood products;
  \item public image-level simulations matching the final survey selection.
\end{itemize}

\clearpage
\section{Conclusion}

The \facility\ is a coherent cosmology mission when it is built around the real strength of slitless spectroscopy. That strength is dense, homogeneous, robotic redshift mapping over large solid angle. The \fovlarge\ option is therefore not an optional upgrade but the enabling survey mode for the ELG BAO/RSD program. The \fovsmall\ mode remains useful for deep calibration, supernova cadence, and targeted quasar work. The supernova program carries a quantified reach of its own, since the rest-frame $U$ and NUV magnitudes that separate the optical twins are measured at subgroup precision to $z\simeq0.9$--$1.1$ in the standard two-hour visits and to $z\simeq1.3$--$1.5$ in ten-hour stacks (Figure~\ref{fig:snuvlimit}), covering the redshift range that anchors the dark-energy measurement. The deep imaging that rides the spectroscopic tiers carries a matching one, since it uses emission-line redshifts to recover the low-surface-brightness galaxies that the team's Horizon Run 5 mock surveys show are missing from the observed stellar mass function at $0.625\le z\le2$ (Figure~\ref{fig:hr5gsmf}). Therefore, the census turns the faint end of that function from a selection-truncated calibration target into a direct test of stellar feedback.

The revised mission statement is:
\begin{leadbox}[title=Mission Statement]{teal}
\textbf{The \facility\ will conduct a band-limited, multi-roll, $R=1000$ slitless spectroscopic survey optimized for ELG BAO/RSD cosmology, supported by supernova and high-redshift quasar programs that exploit the stability and multiplexing of space-based robotic operations.}
\end{leadbox}

\section*{Figures and Sources}
Most schematic figures in this document are generated directly in \LaTeX\ using TikZ. Figure~\ref{fig:crowded} is a slitless scene simulation generated locally at the baseline instrument parameters with the tracked script \texttt{make\_slitless\_scene\_sim.py}, using the repository's FSPS galaxy templates as source spectra. Figure~\ref{fig:elgspectrum} uses an observed bright SDSS DR17 low-redshift emission-line galaxy spectrum converted to a local plotting table. Figure~\ref{fig:elgobswavez} stacks actual SDSS/BOSS and DESI DR1 spectra retrieved through SPARCL together with HST WFC3/G141 3D-HST COSMOS grism spectra (v4.1.5 1D extractions) and JWST/NIRSpec PRISM star-forming galaxy spectra (msaexp 1D extractions from the DAWN JWST Archive) on an approximately uniform display grid in redshift, spanning the full $0.36$--$3.0\,\mu$m proposed range. Figures~\ref{fig:elgmagdepth} and \ref{fig:desiMlf} use the DESI DR1 LSS ELG catalog with an observed-frame absolute-magnitude proxy computed locally from the downloaded FITS table. In Figure~\ref{fig:desiMlf} the binned proxy LF is additionally fit with a Schechter function on the bright side of each redshift bin and shown as smooth curves. Figure~\ref{fig:phillips} plots the real Carnegie Supernova Project\,I sample of 139 SNe Ia (Burns et~al.\ \cite{burns2018csp}, VizieR catalog J/ApJ/869/56), computing the host-extinction-corrected peak absolute magnitude $M_V=V_{\max}-\mu_{\rm CV}-R_V\,E(B\!-\!V)$ from the tabulated distance modulus and reddening, plotting it against the tabulated $\Delta m_{15}(B)$, and overlaying a quadratic Phillips relation fitted locally to those points. The figure layout follows Figure 11 of Zeng et~al.\ \cite{zeng2025sn2023ehl}. Figure~\ref{fig:uvtwins} plots the observed flux-calibrated Swift/UVOT UV-grism spectra of SN\,2011fe and SN\,2011by near maximum, retrieved from the Open Supernova Catalog archive \cite{guillochon2017}, deredshifted and scaled over the optical. Figure~\ref{fig:sniacosmo} (the multi-cosmology SN Ia Hubble diagram with current and target Hubble-residual scatter) is generated locally with \texttt{astropy} distance moduli for the four cosmologies against the real Pantheon$+$SH0ES sample \cite{brout2022}, retaining the 1580 Hubble-flow SNe Ia ($z>0.01$, Cepheid calibrators removed). Figure~\ref{fig:nuvband} is generated in TikZ and shows the redshifting of fixed rest-frame wavelengths into the proposed observed band. Figure~\ref{fig:lyathreshold} is computed locally, integrating a Voigt profile to obtain the Ly$\alpha$ curve of growth and inverting it against the $R=1000$ detection limit of Eq.~\eqref{eq:wmin} to give the minimum detectable H\,\textsc{i} column density. Figure~\ref{fig:lya3dexternal} is an external CLAMATO 3D Ly$\alpha$ forest tomography rendering from the arXiv source package. Figure~\ref{fig:hr4xi} is rendered locally from the author's Horizon Run 4 pair-count measurement, the raw $\xi(\sigma,\pi)$ grids in real and redshift space, rather than from any published figure. Figures~\ref{fig:sidmsigmav} and \ref{fig:fdmslice} are the original figures from the arXiv source packages of Kaplinghat, Tulin \& Yu \cite{kaplinghat2016} and Schive et~al.\ \cite{schive2014}, reproduced as published and marked courtesy in their captions, because the underlying per-system cross-section fits and wave-simulation data are not public. Figure~\ref{fig:sparcdiv} is drawn locally from the public SPARC mass-model files \cite{lelli2016}. Figure~\ref{fig:fdmconstraints} is drawn locally from the published bounds quoted in its caption. Figures~\ref{fig:cirrusfil} and \ref{fig:cirruscolors} are the original figures from the arXiv source package of Rom\'an, Trujillo \& Montes \cite{roman2020}, reproduced as published and marked courtesy in their captions, because the processed deep mosaics and per-field cirrus colour measurements behind them are not public. Figure~\ref{fig:hr5gsmf} is the original figure from the arXiv source package of the team's own Horizon Run 5 stellar-mass-function study \cite{kim2023gsmf}, reproduced as published. Literature and documentation sources are listed below.
